{"uri": "2024-07-416961106", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-10", "time": "05:18:19", "dateTime": "2024-07-10T05:18:19Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-10T01:05:00Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9764705896377563, "url": "https://www.usnews.com/news/us/articles/2024-07-10/las-vegas-eyes-record-of-5th-consecutive-day-over-115-degrees-as-heat-wave-continues-to-scorch-us", "title": "Las Vegas Eyes Record of 5th Consecutive Day Over 115 Degrees as Heat Wave Continues to Scorch US", "body": "LAS VEGAS (AP) -- Used to shrugging off the heat, Las Vegas residents are now eyeing the thermometer as the desert city is on track Wednesday to set a record for the most consecutive days over 115 degrees Fahrenheit (46.1 Celsius) amid a lingering hot spell that will continue scorching much of the U.S. into the weekend.\n\nOn Tuesday, Las Vegas flirted again with the all-time temperature record of 120 F (48.8 C) reached on Sunday, but settled for a new daily mark of 119 F (48.3 C) that smashed the old one of 116 F (46.6 C) set for the date in 2021. Forecasters say the city will likely hit a record fifth straight day above 115 F (46.1 C) on Wednesday.\n\nEven by desert standards, the prolonged baking that Nevada's largest city is experiencing is nearly unprecedented.\n\n\"This is the most extreme heat wave in the history of record-keeping in Las Vegas since 1937,\" said meteorologist John Adair, a veteran of three decades at the National Weather Service office in southern Nevada.\n\nTuesday's high temperature tied the mark of four straight days above 115 F (46.1 C) set in July 2005. And Adair said the record could be extended through Friday.\n\nAlyse Sobosan said this July has been the hottest in the 15 years she has lived in Las Vegas. A counselor at a charter school that's on summer break, Sobosan said she doesn't step outside during the day if she can help it, and waits until 9 p.m. or later to walk her dogs.\n\n\"It's oppressively hot,\" she said. \"It's like you can't really live your life.\"\n\nIt's also dangerously hot, health officials have emphasized.\n\n\"Even people of average age who are seemingly healthy can suffer heat illness when it's so hot its hard for your body to cool down,\" said Alexis Brignola, an epidemiologist at the Southern Nevada Health District.\n\nThe searing heat wave gripping large parts of the U.S. also led to record daily high temperatures in Oregon, where it is suspected to have caused six deaths, the state medical examiner's office said Tuesday. More than 161 million people around the U.S. were under heat alerts Tuesday, especially in Western states.\n\nDozens of locations across the West tied or broke previous heat records over the weekend and are expected to keep doing so all week.\n\nThe heat was blamed for a motorcyclist's death over the weekend in Death Valley National Park. At Death Valley on Tuesday, tourists queued for photos in front of a giant thermometer that was reading 120 F (48.9 C).\n\nSimon Pell and Lisa Gregory from London left their air-conditioned RV to experience a mid-day blast of heat that would be unthinkable back home.\n\n\"I don't need a thermometer to tell me that it's hot,\" Pell said. \"You hear about it in stories and and wildlife documentaries. But just for me, I wanted to experience what it would feel like. ... It's an incredible experience.\"\n\nDeath Valley is considered one of the most extreme environments in the world. The hottest temperature ever officially recorded on Earth was 134 F (56.67 C) in July 1913 in Death Valley, though some experts dispute that measurement and say the real record was 130 F (54.4 C), recorded there in July 2021.\n\nRecord highs for the date were also hit Tuesday in parts of Oregon and Washington, with Portland reaching 103 F (39.4 C) and Salem and Eugene hitting 105 F (40.5 C). Triple digit temperatures were also recorded in Idaho.\n\nPhoenix, which has averaged the hottest temperature ever for the first eight days of July in records dating to 1885, tied the daily record Tuesday of 116 F (46.6) set in 1958.\n\nThe high Tuesday of 106 F (41.1 C) in Reno, Nevada, broke the daily record of 104 F (40 C) last tied in 2017 and extended to four days the longest streak ever of 105 F (40.5 C) or higher. Before this week, at an elevation of 4,500 feet (1,372 meters), the city had never been that hot for more than two consecutive days in records dating to 1888.\n\nThe U.S. heat wave came as the global temperature in June was a record warm for the 13th straight month and marked the 12th straight month that the world was 1.5 degrees Celsius (2.7 degrees Fahrenheit) warmer than pre-industrial times, the European climate service Copernicus said. Most of this heat, trapped by human-caused climate change, is from long-term warming from greenhouse gases emitted by the burning of coal, oil and natural gas, scientists say.\n\nIn Las Vegas, hotels and casinos keep their visitors cool with massive AC units. But for homeless residents and others without access to safe environments, officials have set up emergency cooling centers at community centers across southern Nevada.\n\nFirefighters in Henderson, Nevada, last week became the first in the region to deploy what city spokesperson Madeleine Skains called \"polar pods\" used to cool a person exhibiting symptoms of heat stroke or a related medical emergency.\n\nSkains said four vehicles, including battalion chiefs in the city of more than 330,000 residents have the devices that are similar to units first put into use a month ago in Phoenix. They can be filled with water and ice to immerse a medical patient in cold water on the way to a hospital.\n\nExtreme heat and a longstanding drought in the West has also dried out vegetation that fuels wildfires.\n\nA new blaze in Oregon, dubbed the Larch Creek Fire, quickly grew to more than 5 square miles (12 square kilometers) Tuesday evening as flames tore through grassland in Wasco County. Evacuations were ordered for remote homes about 15 miles (24 kilometers) south of The Dalles.\n\nIn California, firefighters were battling least 18 wildfires Tuesday, including a 42-square-mile (109-square-kilometer) blaze that prompted evacuation orders for about 200 residences in the mountains of Santa Barbara County. The Lake Fire was only 16% contained, and forecasters warned of a \"volatile combination\" of high heat, low humidity and northwest winds developing late in the day.\n\nNortheast of Los Angeles, the 2-square-mile (5-square-kilometer) Vista Fire chewed through trees in the San Bernardino National Forest and sent up a huge plume of smoke visible across the region.\n\nThe National Weather Service said it was extending the excessive heat warnings across most of the Southwest U.S. through Saturday morning.\n\nWeber reported from Los Angeles. AP journalists Anita Snow in Phoenix; Ty O'Neil in Death Valley, California; Rio Yamat in Las Vegas; and Scott Sonner and Gabe Stern in Reno, Nevada, contributed to this report.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. {"uri": "2024-07-416968238", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-10", "time": "05:26:14", "dateTime": "2024-07-10T05:26:14Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-10T05:05:23Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9764705896377563, "url": "https://www.wboc.com/news/national/las-vegas-eyes-record-of-5th-consecutive-day-over-115-degrees-as-heat-wave-continues/article_2a9cdb2e-edc3-57f5-b872-67a69801ec96.html", "title": "Las Vegas eyes record of 5th consecutive day over 115 degrees as heat wave continues to scorch US", "body": "David Clarke who is suffering homelessness and living in his car with his 6 dogs, takes to the shade at the Sepulveda Basin dog park in Los Angeles on Tuesday, July 9, 2024. Dozens of locations in the West and Pacific Northwest tied or broke previous heat records over the weekend and are expected to keep doing so into the week.\n\nLAS VEGAS (AP) -- Used to shrugging off the heat, Las Vegas residents are now eyeing the thermometer as the desert city is on track Wednesday to set a record for the most consecutive days over 115 degrees Fahrenheit (46.1 Celsius) amid a lingering hot spell that will continue scorching much of the U.S. into the weekend.\n\nOn Tuesday, Las Vegas flirted again with the all-time temperature record of 120 F (48.8 C) reached on Sunday, but settled for a new daily mark of 119 F (48.3 C) that smashed the old one of 116 F (46.6 C) set for the date in 2021. Forecasters say the city will likely hit a record fifth straight day above 115 F (46.1 C) on Wednesday.\n\nEven by desert standards, the prolonged baking that Nevada's largest city is experiencing is nearly unprecedented.\n\n\"This is the most extreme heat wave in the history of record-keeping in Las Vegas since 1937,\" said meteorologist John Adair, a veteran of three decades at the National Weather Service office in southern Nevada.\n\nTuesday's high temperature tied the mark of four straight days above 115 F (46.1 C) set in July 2005. And Adair said the record could be extended through Friday.\n\nAlyse Sobosan said this July has been the hottest in the 15 years she has lived in Las Vegas. A counselor at a charter school that's on summer break, Sobosan said she doesn't step outside during the day if she can help it, and waits until 9 p.m. or later to walk her dogs.\n\n\"It's oppressively hot,\" she said. \"It's like you can't really live your life.\"\n\nIt's also dangerously hot, health officials have emphasized.\n\n\"Even people of average age who are seemingly healthy can suffer heat illness when it's so hot its hard for your body to cool down,\" said Alexis Brignola, an epidemiologist at the Southern Nevada Health District.\n\nThe searing heat wave gripping large parts of the U.S. also led to record daily high temperatures in Oregon, where it is suspected to have caused six deaths, the state medical examiner's office said Tuesday. More than 161 million people around the U.S. were under heat alerts Tuesday, especially in Western states.\n\nDozens of locations across the West tied or broke previous heat records over the weekend and are expected to keep doing so all week.\n\nThe heat was blamed for a motorcyclist's death over the weekend in Death Valley National Park. At Death Valley on Tuesday, tourists queued for photos in front of a giant thermometer that was reading 120 F (48.9 C).\n\nSimon Pell and Lisa Gregory from London left their air-conditioned RV to experience a mid-day blast of heat that would be unthinkable back home.\n\n\"I don't need a thermometer to tell me that it's hot,\" Pell said. \"You hear about it in stories and and wildlife documentaries. But just for me, I wanted to experience what it would feel like. ... It's an incredible experience.\"\n\nDeath Valley is considered one of the most extreme environments in the world. The hottest temperature ever officially recorded on Earth was 134 F (56.67 C) in July 1913 in Death Valley, though some experts dispute that measurement and say the real record was 130 F (54.4 C), recorded there in July 2021.\n\nRecord highs for the date were also hit Tuesday in parts of Oregon and Washington, with Portland reaching 103 F (39.4 C) and Salem and Eugene hitting 105 F (40.5 C). Triple digit temperatures were also recorded in Idaho.\n\nPhoenix, which has averaged the hottest temperature ever for the first eight days of July in records dating to 1885, tied the daily record Tuesday of 116 F (46.6) set in 1958.\n\nThe high Tuesday of 106 F (41.1 C) in Reno, Nevada, broke the daily record of 104 F (40 C) last tied in 2017 and extended to four days the longest streak ever of 105 F (40.5 C) or higher. Before this week, at an elevation of 4,500 feet (1,372 meters), the city had never been that hot for more than two consecutive days in records dating to 1888.\n\nThe U.S. heat wave came as the global temperature in June was a record warm for the 13th straight month and marked the 12th straight month that the world was 1.5 degrees Celsius (2.7 degrees Fahrenheit) warmer than pre-industrial times, the European climate service Copernicus said. Most of this heat, trapped by human-caused climate change, is from long-term warming from greenhouse gases emitted by the burning of coal, oil and natural gas, scientists say.\n\nIn Las Vegas, hotels and casinos keep their visitors cool with massive AC units. But for homeless residents and others without access to safe environments, officials have set up emergency cooling centers at community centers across southern Nevada.\n\nFirefighters in Henderson, Nevada, last week became the first in the region to deploy what city spokesperson Madeleine Skains called \"polar pods\" used to cool a person exhibiting symptoms of heat stroke or a related medical emergency.\n\nSkains said four vehicles, including battalion chiefs in the city of more than 330,000 residents have the devices that are similar to units first put into use a month ago in Phoenix. They can be filled with water and ice to immerse a medical patient in cold water on the way to a hospital.\n\nExtreme heat and a longstanding drought in the West has also dried out vegetation that fuels wildfires.\n\nA new blaze in Oregon, dubbed the Larch Creek Fire, quickly grew to more than 5 square miles (12 square kilometers) Tuesday evening as flames tore through grassland in Wasco County. Evacuations were ordered for remote homes about 15 miles (24 kilometers) south of The Dalles.\n\nIn California, firefighters were battling least 18 wildfires Tuesday, including a 42-square-mile (109-square-kilometer) blaze that prompted evacuation orders for about 200 residences in the mountains of Santa Barbara County. The Lake Fire was only 16% contained, and forecasters warned of a \"volatile combination\" of high heat, low humidity and northwest winds developing late in the day.\n\nNortheast of Los Angeles, the 2-square-mile (5-square-kilometer) Vista Fire chewed through trees in the San Bernardino National Forest and sent up a huge plume of smoke visible across the region.\n\nThe National Weather Service said it was extending the excessive heat warnings across most of the Southwest U.S. through Saturday morning.\n\n\"It's not over yet,\" the service in Reno said.\n\nWeber reported from Los Angeles. AP journalists Anita Snow in Phoenix; Ty O'Neil in Death Valley, California; Rio Yamat in Las Vegas; and Scott Sonner and Gabe Stern in Reno, Nevada, contributed to this report.", "source": {"uri": And Adair said the record could be extended through Friday.\n\nAlyse Sobosan said this July has been the hottest in the 15 years she has lived in Las Vegas. More than 161 million people around the U.S. were under heat alerts Tuesday, especially in Western states.\n\nDozens of locations across the West tied or broke previous heat records over the weekend and are expected to keep doing so all week.\n\nThe heat was blamed for a motorcyclist's death over the weekend in Death Valley National Park. But just for me, I wanted to experience what it would feel like. ... It's an incredible experience.\"\n\nDeath Valley is considered one of the most extreme environments in the world. The hottest temperature ever officially recorded on Earth was 134 F (56.67 C) in July 1913 in Death Valley, though some experts dispute that measurement and say the real record was 130 F (54.4 C), recorded there in July 2021.\n\nRecord highs for the date were also hit Tuesday in parts of Oregon and Washington, with Portland reaching 103 F (39.4 C) and Salem and Eugene hitting 105 F (40.5 C). Triple digit temperatures were also recorded in Idaho.\n\nPhoenix, which has averaged the hottest temperature ever for the first eight days of July in records dating to 1885, tied the daily record Tuesday of 116 F (46.6) set in 1958.\n\nThe high Tuesday of 106 F (41.1 C) in Reno, Nevada, broke the daily record of 104 F (40 C) last tied in 2017 and extended to four days the longest streak ever of 105 F (40.5 C) or higher. Before this week, at an elevation of 4,500 feet (1,372 meters), the city had never been that hot for more than two consecutive days in records dating to 1888.\n\nThe U.S. heat wave came as the global temperature in June was a record warm for the 13th straight month and marked the 12th straight month that the world was 1.5 degrees Celsius (2.7 degrees Fahrenheit) warmer than pre-industrial times, the European climate service Copernicus said. Most of this heat, trapped by human-caused climate change, is from long-term warming from greenhouse gases emitted by the burning of coal, oil and natural gas, scientists say.\n\nIn Las Vegas, hotels and casinos keep their visitors cool with massive AC units. But for homeless residents and others without access to safe environments, officials have set up emergency cooling centers at community centers across southern Nevada.\n\nFirefighters in Henderson, Nevada, last week became the first in the region to deploy what city spokesperson Madeleine Skains called \"polar pods\" used to cool a person exhibiting symptoms of heat stroke or a related medical emergency.\n\nSkains said four vehicles, including battalion chiefs in the city of more than 330,000 residents have the devices that are similar to units first put into use a month ago in Phoenix. {"uri": "8218371898", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-10", "time": "09:13:31", "dateTime": "2024-07-10T09:13:31Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-10T09:12:52Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9725490212440491, "url": "https://lasvegassun.com/news/2024/jul/10/las-vegas-eyes-record-of-5th-consecutive-day-over/", "title": "Las Vegas eyes record of 5th consecutive day over 115 degrees as heat wave continues to scorch US", "body": "John Locher / AP, file\n\nPeople cool off in misters along the Las Vegas Strip, Sunday, July 7, 2024, in Las Vegas. Used to shrugging off the heat, Las Vegas residents are now eyeing the thermometer as the desert city is on track Wednesday to set a record for the most consecutive days over 115 degrees (46.1 C) amid a lingering hot spell that's expected to continue scorching much of the U.S. into the weekend.\n\nBy Ken Ritter and Scott Sonner, Associated Press\n\nWednesday, July 10, 2024 | 2 a.m.\n\nUsed to shrugging off the heat, Las Vegas residents are now eyeing the thermometer as the desert city is on track Wednesday to set a record for the most consecutive days over 115 degrees Fahrenheit (46.1 Celsius) amid a lingering hot spell that will continue scorching much of the U.S. into the weekend.\n\nOn Tuesday, Las Vegas flirted again with the all-time temperature record of 120 F (48.8 C) reached on Sunday, but settled for a new daily mark of 119 F (48.3 C) that smashed the old one of 116 F (46.6 C) set for the date in 2021. Forecasters say the city will likely hit a record fifth straight day above 115 F (46.1 C) on Wednesday.\n\nEven by desert standards, the prolonged baking that Nevada's largest city is experiencing is nearly unprecedented.\n\n\"This is the most extreme heat wave in the history of record-keeping in Las Vegas since 1937,\" said meteorologist John Adair, a veteran of three decades at the National Weather Service office in southern Nevada.\n\nTuesday's high temperature tied the mark of four straight days above 115 F (46.1 C) set in July 2005. And Adair said the record could be extended through Friday.\n\nAlyse Sobosan said this July has been the hottest in the 15 years she has lived in Las Vegas. A counselor at a charter school that's on summer break, Sobosan said she doesn't step outside during the day if she can help it, and waits until 9 p.m. or later to walk her dogs.\n\n\"It's oppressively hot,\" she said. \"It's like you can't really live your life.\"\n\nIt's also dangerously hot, health officials have emphasized.\n\n\"Even people of average age who are seemingly healthy can suffer heat illness when it's so hot its hard for your body to cool down,\" said Alexis Brignola, an epidemiologist at the Southern Nevada Health District.\n\nThe searing heat wave gripping large parts of the U.S. also led to record daily high temperatures in Oregon, where it is suspected to have caused six deaths, the state medical examiner's office said Tuesday. More than 161 million people around the U.S. were under heat alerts Tuesday, especially in Western states.\n\nDozens of locations across the West tied or broke previous heat records over the weekend and are expected to keep doing so all week.\n\nThe heat was blamed for a motorcyclist's death over the weekend in Death Valley National Park. At Death Valley on Tuesday, tourists queued for photos in front of a giant thermometer that was reading 120 F (48.9 C).\n\nSimon Pell and Lisa Gregory from London left their air-conditioned RV to experience a mid-day blast of heat that would be unthinkable back home.\n\n\"I don't need a thermometer to tell me that it's hot,\" Pell said. \"You hear about it in stories and and wildlife documentaries. But just for me, I wanted to experience what it would feel like. ... It's an incredible experience.\"\n\nDeath Valley is considered one of the most extreme environments in the world. The hottest temperature ever officially recorded on Earth was 134 F (56.67 C) in July 1913 in Death Valley, though some experts dispute that measurement and say the real record was 130 F (54.4 C), recorded there in July 2021.\n\nRecord highs for the date were also hit Tuesday in parts of Oregon and Washington, with Portland reaching 103 F (39.4 C) and Salem and Eugene hitting 105 F (40.5 C). Triple digit temperatures were also recorded in Idaho.\n\nPhoenix, which has averaged the hottest temperature ever for the first eight days of July in records dating to 1885, tied the daily record Tuesday of 116 F (46.6) set in 1958.\n\nThe high Tuesday of 106 F (41.1 C) in Reno, Nevada, broke the daily record of 104 F (40 C) last tied in 2017 and extended to four days the longest streak ever of 105 F (40.5 C) or higher. Before this week, at an elevation of 4,500 feet (1,372 meters), the city had never been that hot for more than two consecutive days in records dating to 1888.\n\nThe U.S. heat wave came as the global temperature in June was a record warm for the 13th straight month and marked the 12th straight month that the world was 1.5 degrees Celsius (2.7 degrees Fahrenheit) warmer than pre-industrial times, the European climate service Copernicus said. Most of this heat, trapped by human-caused climate change, is from long-term warming from greenhouse gases emitted by the burning of coal, oil and natural gas, scientists say.\n\nIn Las Vegas, hotels and casinos keep their visitors cool with massive AC units. But for homeless residents and others without access to safe environments, officials have set up emergency cooling centers at community centers across southern Nevada.\n\nFirefighters in Henderson, Nevada, last week became the first in the region to deploy what city spokesperson Madeleine Skains called \"polar pods\" used to cool a person exhibiting symptoms of heat stroke or a related medical emergency.\n\nSkains said four vehicles, including battalion chiefs in the city of more than 330,000 residents have the devices that are similar to units first put into use a month ago in Phoenix. They can be filled with water and ice to immerse a medical patient in cold water on the way to a hospital.\n\nExtreme heat and a longstanding drought in the West has also dried out vegetation that fuels wildfires.\n\nA new blaze in Oregon, dubbed the Larch Creek Fire, quickly grew to more than 5 square miles (12 square kilometers) Tuesday evening as flames tore through grassland in Wasco County. Evacuations were ordered for remote homes about 15 miles (24 kilometers) south of The Dalles.\n\nIn California, firefighters were battling least 18 wildfires Tuesday, including a 42-square-mile (109-square-kilometer) blaze that prompted evacuation orders for about 200 residences in the mountains of Santa Barbara County. The Lake Fire was only 16% contained, and forecasters warned of a \"volatile combination\" of high heat, low humidity and northwest winds developing late in the day.\n\nNortheast of Los Angeles, the 2-square-mile (5-square-kilometer) Vista Fire chewed through trees in the San Bernardino National Forest and sent up a huge plume of smoke visible across the region.\n\nThe National Weather Service said it was extending the excessive heat warnings across most of the Southwest U.S. through Saturday morning.\n\n\"It's not over yet,\" the service in Reno said.\n\n___\n\nWeber reported from Los Angeles. AP journalists Anita Snow in Phoenix; Ty O'Neil in Death Valley, California; Rio Yamat in Las Vegas; and Scott Sonner and Gabe Stern in Reno contributed to this report.", "source": {"uri": {"uri": "8218951403", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-10", "time": "15:09:55", "dateTime": "2024-07-10T15:09:55Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-10T15:09:30Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9647058844566345, "url": "https://abc7.com/post/las-vegas-eyes-record-5th-consecutive-day-115/15048650/", "title": "Las Vegas eyes record of 5th consecutive day over 115 degrees as heat wave continues to scorch US", "body": "On Sunday, temperatures in Las Vegas hit 120 degrees for the first time in recorded history, according to preliminary data from the weather service.\n\nUsed to shrugging off the heat, Las Vegas residents are now eyeing the thermometer as the desert city is on track Wednesday to set a record for the most consecutive days over 115 degrees Fahrenheit (46.1 Celsius) amid a lingering hot spell that will continue scorching much of the U.S. into the weekend.\n\nOn Tuesday, Las Vegas flirted again with the all-time temperature record of 120 F (48.8 C) reached on Sunday, but settled for a new daily mark of 119 F (48.3 C) that smashed the old one of 116 F (46.6 C) set for the date in 2021. Forecasters say the city will likely hit a record fifth straight day above 115 F (46.1 C) on Wednesday.\n\nEven by desert standards, the prolonged baking that Nevada's largest city is experiencing is nearly unprecedented.\n\n\"This is the most extreme heat wave in the history of record-keeping in Las Vegas since 1937,\" said meteorologist John Adair, a veteran of three decades at the National Weather Service office in southern Nevada.\n\nTuesday's high temperature tied the mark of four straight days above 115 F (46.1 C) set in July 2005. And Adair said the record could be extended through Friday.\n\nAlyse Sobosan said this July has been the hottest in the 15 years she has lived in Las Vegas. A counselor at a charter school that's on summer break, Sobosan said she doesn't step outside during the day if she can help it, and waits until 9 p.m. or later to walk her dogs.\n\n\"It's oppressively hot,\" she said. \"It's like you can't really live your life.\"\n\nIt's also dangerously hot, health officials have emphasized.\n\n\"Even people of average age who are seemingly healthy can suffer heat illness when it's so hot its hard for your body to cool down,\" said Alexis Brignola, an epidemiologist at the Southern Nevada Health District.\n\nThe searing heat wave gripping large parts of the U.S. also led to record daily high temperatures in Oregon, where it is suspected to have caused six deaths, the state medical examiner's office said Tuesday. More than 161 million people around the U.S. were under heat alerts Tuesday, especially in Western states.\n\nDozens of locations across the West tied or broke previous heat records over the weekend and are expected to keep doing so all week.\n\nThe heat was blamed for a motorcyclist's death over the weekend in Death Valley National Park. At Death Valley on Tuesday, tourists queued for photos in front of a giant thermometer that was reading 120 F (48.9 C).\n\nSimon Pell and Lisa Gregory from London left their air-conditioned RV to experience a mid-day blast of heat that would be unthinkable back home.\n\n\"I don't need a thermometer to tell me that it's hot,\" Pell said. \"You hear about it in stories and and wildlife documentaries. But just for me, I wanted to experience what it would feel like. ... It's an incredible experience.\"\n\nDeath Valley is considered one of the most extreme environments in the world. The hottest temperature ever officially recorded on Earth was 134 F (56.67 C) in July 1913 in Death Valley, though some experts dispute that measurement and say the real record was 130 F (54.4 C), recorded there in July 2021.\n\nRecord highs for the date were also hit Tuesday in parts of Oregon and Washington, with Portland reaching 103 F (39.4 C) and Salem and Eugene hitting 105 F (40.5 C). Triple digit temperatures were also recorded in Idaho.\n\nPhoenix, which has averaged the hottest temperature ever for the first eight days of July in records dating to 1885, tied the daily record Tuesday of 116 F (46.6) set in 1958.\n\nThe high Tuesday of 106 F (41.1 C) in Reno, Nevada, broke the daily record of 104 F (40 C) last tied in 2017 and extended to four days the longest streak ever of 105 F (40.5 C) or higher. Before this week, at an elevation of 4,500 feet (1,372 meters), the city had never been that hot for more than two consecutive days in records dating to 1888.\n\nThe U.S. heat wave came as the global temperature in June was a record warm for the 13th straight month and marked the 12th straight month that the world was 1.5 degrees Celsius (2.7 degrees Fahrenheit) warmer than pre-industrial times, the European climate service Copernicus said. Most of this heat, trapped by human-caused climate change, is from long-term warming from greenhouse gases emitted by the burning of coal, oil and natural gas, scientists say.\n\nIn Las Vegas, hotels and casinos keep their visitors cool with massive AC units. But for homeless residents and others without access to safe environments, officials have set up emergency cooling centers at community centers across southern Nevada.\n\nFirefighters in Henderson, Nevada, last week became the first in the region to deploy what city spokesperson Madeleine Skains called \"polar pods\" used to cool a person exhibiting symptoms of heat stroke or a related medical emergency.\n\nSkains said four vehicles, including battalion chiefs in the city of more than 330,000 residents have the devices that are similar to units first put into use a month ago in Phoenix. They can be filled with water and ice to immerse a medical patient in cold water on the way to a hospital.\n\nExtreme heat and a longstanding drought in the West has also dried out vegetation that fuels wildfires.\n\nA new blaze in Oregon, dubbed the Larch Creek Fire, quickly grew to more than 5 square miles (12 square kilometers) Tuesday evening as flames tore through grassland in Wasco County. Evacuations were ordered for remote homes about 15 miles (24 kilometers) south of The Dalles.\n\nIn California, firefighters were battling least 18 wildfires Tuesday, including a 42-square-mile (109-square-kilometer) blaze that prompted evacuation orders for about 200 residences in the mountains of Santa Barbara County. The Lake Fire was only 16% contained, and forecasters warned of a \"volatile combination\" of high heat, low humidity and northwest winds developing late in the day.\n\nNortheast of Los Angeles, the 2-square-mile (5-square-kilometer) Vista Fire chewed through trees in the San Bernardino National Forest and sent up a huge plume of smoke visible across the region.\n\nThe National Weather Service said it was extending the excessive heat warnings across most of the Southwest U.S. through Saturday morning.\n\nWeber reported from Los Angeles. More than 161 million people around the U.S. were under heat alerts Tuesday, especially in Western states.\n\nDozens of locations across the West tied or broke previous heat records over the weekend and are expected to keep doing so all week.\n\nRELATED STORY | Scorching heat wave creates deadly conditions in national parks\n\nThe heat was blamed for a motorcyclist's death over the weekend in Death Valley National Park. At Death Valley on Tuesday, tourists queued for photos in front of a giant thermometer that was reading 120 F.\n\nSimon Pell and Lisa Gregory from London left their air-conditioned RV to experience a mid-day blast of heat that would be unthinkable back home.\n\n\"I don't need a thermometer to tell me that it's hot,\" Pell said. \"You hear about it in stories and wildlife documentaries. But just for me, I wanted to experience what it would feel like. ... It's an incredible experience.\"\n\nDeath Valley is considered one of the most extreme environments in the world. The hottest temperature ever officially recorded on Earth was 134 F in July 1913 in Death Valley, though some experts dispute that measurement and say the real record was 130 F, recorded there in July 2021.\n\nRecord highs for the date were also hit Tuesday in parts of Oregon and Washington, with Portland reaching 103 F and Salem and Eugene hitting 105 F. Triple digit temperatures were also recorded in Idaho.\n\nPhoenix, which has averaged the hottest temperature ever for the first eight days of July in records dating to 1885, tied the daily record Tuesday of 116 F set in 1958.\n\nThe high Tuesday of 106 F in Reno, Nevada, broke the daily record of 104 F last tied in 2017 and extended to four days the longest streak ever of 105 F or higher. Before this week, at an elevation of 4,500 feet, the city had never been that hot for more than two consecutive days in records dating to 1888.\n\nThe U.S. heat wave came as the global temperature in June was a record warm for the 13th straight month and marked the 12th straight month that the world was 2.7 degrees Fahrenheit warmer than pre-industrial times, the European climate service Copernicus said. Most of this heat, trapped by human-caused climate change, is from long-term warming from greenhouse gases emitted by the burning of coal, oil and natural gas, scientists say.\n\nIn Las Vegas, hotels and casinos keep their visitors cool with massive AC units. But for homeless residents and others without access to safe environments, officials have set up emergency cooling centers at community centers across southern Nevada.\n\nFirefighters in Henderson, Nevada, last week became the first in the region to deploy what city spokesperson Madeleine Skains called \"polar pods\" used to cool a person exhibiting symptoms of heat stroke or a related medical emergency.\n\nSkains said four vehicles, including battalion chiefs in the city of more than 330,000 residents have the devices that are similar to units first put into use a month ago in Phoenix. They can be filled with water and ice to immerse a medical patient in cold water on the way to a hospital.\n\nExtreme heat and a longstanding drought in the West has also dried out vegetation that fuels wildfires.\n\nA new blaze in Oregon, dubbed the Larch Creek Fire, quickly grew to more than 5 square miles Tuesday evening as flames tore through grassland in Wasco County. Evacuations were ordered for remote homes about 15 miles south of The Dalles.\n\nIn California, firefighters were battling least 18 wildfires Tuesday, including a 42-square-mile blaze that prompted evacuation orders for about 200 residences in the mountains of Santa Barbara County. The Lake Fire was only 16% contained, and forecasters warned of a \"volatile combination\" of high heat, low humidity and northwest winds developing late in the day.\n\nNortheast of Los Angeles, the 2-square-mile Vista Fire chewed through trees in the San Bernardino National Forest and sent up a huge plume of smoke visible across the region.\n\nThe National Weather Service said it was extending {"uri": "8218817728", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-10", "time": "13:44:38", "dateTime": "2024-07-10T13:44:38Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-10T13:44:03Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9647058844566345, "url": "https://www.pbs.org/newshour/nation/las-vegas-under-most-extreme-heat-wave-in-recorded-history-meteorologist-says", "title": "Las Vegas under 'most extreme heat wave' in recorded history, meteorologist says", "body": "LAS VEGAS (AP) -- Used to shrugging off the heat, Las Vegas residents are now eyeing the thermometer as the desert city is on track Wednesday to set a record for the most consecutive days over 115 degrees Fahrenheit (46.1 Celsius) amid a lingering hot spell that will continue scorching much of the U.S. into the weekend.\n\nREAD MORE: How discriminatory housing policies of the past are shaping heat waves today in minority and low-income neighborhoods\n\nOn Tuesday, Las Vegas flirted again with the all-time temperature record of 120 F (48.8 C) reached on Sunday, but settled for a new daily mark of 119 F (48.3 C) that smashed the old one of 116 F (46.6 C) set for the date in 2021. Forecasters say the city will likely hit a record fifth straight day above 115 F (46.1 C) on Wednesday.\n\nEven by desert standards, the prolonged baking that Nevada's largest city is experiencing is nearly unprecedented.\n\n\"This is the most extreme heat wave in the history of record-keeping in Las Vegas since 1937,\" said meteorologist John Adair, a veteran of three decades at the National Weather Service office in southern Nevada.\n\nTuesday's high temperature tied the mark of four straight days above 115 F (46.1 C) set in July 2005. And Adair said the record could be extended through Friday.\n\nAlyse Sobosan said this July has been the hottest in the 15 years she has lived in Las Vegas. A counselor at a charter school that's on summer break, Sobosan said she doesn't step outside during the day if she can help it, and waits until 9 p.m. or later to walk her dogs.\n\n\"It's oppressively hot,\" she said. \"It's like you can't really live your life.\"\n\nIt's also dangerously hot, health officials have emphasized.\n\n\"Even people of average age who are seemingly healthy can suffer heat illness when it's so hot its hard for your body to cool down,\" said Alexis Brignola, an epidemiologist at the Southern Nevada Health District.\n\nThe searing heat wave gripping large parts of the U.S. also led to record daily high temperatures in Oregon, where it is suspected to have caused six deaths, the state medical examiner's office said Tuesday. More than 161 million people around the U.S. were under heat alerts Tuesday, especially in Western states.\n\nDozens of locations across the West tied or broke previous heat records over the weekend and are expected to keep doing so all week.\n\nThe heat was blamed for a motorcyclist's death over the weekend in Death Valley National Park. At Death Valley on Tuesday, tourists queued for photos in front of a giant thermometer that was reading 120 F (48.9 C).\n\nSimon Pell and Lisa Gregory from London left their air-conditioned RV to experience a mid-day blast of heat that would be unthinkable back home.\n\n\"I don't need a thermometer to tell me that it's hot,\" Pell said. \"You hear about it in stories and and wildlife documentaries. But just for me, I wanted to experience what it would feel like. ... It's an incredible experience.\"\n\nDeath Valley is considered one of the most extreme environments in the world. The hottest temperature ever officially recorded on Earth was 134 F (56.67 C) in July 1913 in Death Valley, though some experts dispute that measurement and say the real record was 130 F (54.4 C), recorded there in July 2021.\n\nRecord highs for the date were also hit Tuesday in parts of Oregon and Washington, with Portland reaching 103 F (39.4 C) and Salem and Eugene hitting 105 F (40.5 C). Triple digit temperatures were also recorded in Idaho.\n\nPhoenix, which has averaged the hottest temperature ever for the first eight days of July in records dating to 1885, tied the daily record Tuesday of 116 F (46.6) set in 1958.\n\nThe high Tuesday of 106 F (41.1 C) in Reno, Nevada, broke the daily record of 104 F (40 C) last tied in 2017 and extended to four days the longest streak ever of 105 F (40.5 C) or higher. Before this week, at an elevation of 4,500 feet (1,372 meters), the city had never been that hot for more than two consecutive days in records dating to 1888.\n\nREAD MORE: Summer camps are adapting outdoor activities to protect kids during the heat wave\n\nThe U.S. heat wave came as the global temperature in June was a record warm for the 13th straight month and marked the 12th straight month that the world was 1.5 degrees Celsius (2.7 degrees Fahrenheit) warmer than pre-industrial times, the European climate service Copernicus said. Most of this heat, trapped by human-caused climate change, is from long-term warming from greenhouse gases emitted by the burning of coal, oil and natural gas, scientists say.\n\nIn Las Vegas, hotels and casinos keep their visitors cool with massive AC units. But for homeless residents and others without access to safe environments, officials have set up emergency cooling centers at community centers across southern Nevada.\n\nFirefighters in Henderson, Nevada, last week became the first in the region to deploy what city spokesperson Madeleine Skains called \"polar pods\" used to cool a person exhibiting symptoms of heat stroke or a related medical emergency.\n\nSkains said four vehicles, including battalion chiefs in the city of more than 330,000 residents have the devices that are similar to units first put into use a month ago in Phoenix. They can be filled with water and ice to immerse a medical patient in cold water on the way to a hospital.\n\nExtreme heat and a longstanding drought in the West has also dried out vegetation that fuels wildfires.\n\nA new blaze in Oregon, dubbed the Larch Creek Fire, quickly grew to more than 5 square miles (12 square kilometers) Tuesday evening as flames tore through grassland in Wasco County. Evacuations were ordered for remote homes about 15 miles (24 kilometers) south of The Dalles.\n\nIn California, firefighters were battling least 18 wildfires Tuesday, including a 42-square-mile (109-square-kilometer) blaze that prompted evacuation orders for about 200 residences in the mountains of Santa Barbara County. The Lake Fire was only 16 percent contained, and forecasters warned of a \"volatile combination\" of high heat, low humidity and northwest winds developing late in the day.\n\nNortheast of Los Angeles, the 2-square-mile (5-square-kilometer) Vista Fire chewed through trees in the San Bernardino National Forest and sent up a huge plume of smoke visible across the region.\n\nThe National Weather Service said it was extending the excessive heat warnings across most of the Southwest U.S. through Saturday morning.\n\n\"It's not over yet,\" the service in Reno said.\n\nWeber reported from Los Angeles. {"uri": "8218712319", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-10", "time": "12:41:34", "dateTime": "2024-07-10T12:41:34Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-10T12:40:20Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.95686274766922, "url": "https://businessmirror.com.ph/2024/07/10/las-vegas-eyes-record-5th-consecutive-day-over-115-degrees-as-heat-wave-continues-to-sear-us/", "title": "Las Vegas eyes record 5th consecutive day over 115 degrees as heat wave continues to sear US | BusinessMirror", "body": "LAS VEGAS -- Used to shrugging off the heat, Las Vegas residents are now eyeing the thermometer as the desert city is on track Wednesday to set a record for the most consecutive days over 115 degrees Fahrenheit (46.1\u00b0Celsius) amid a lingering hot spell that will continue scorching much of the US into the weekend.\n\nOn Tuesday, Las Vegas flirted again with the all-time temperature record of 120 F (48.8\u00b0C) reached on Sunday, but settled for a new daily mark of 119 F (48.3\u00b0C) that smashed the old one of 116\u00b0F (46.6\u00b0C) set for the date in 2021. Forecasters say the city will likely hit a record fifth straight day above 115 F (46.1\u00b0C) on Wednesday.\n\nEven by desert standards, the prolonged baking that Nevada's largest city is experiencing is nearly unprecedented.\n\n\"This is the most extreme heat wave in the history of record-keeping in Las Vegas since 1937,\" said meteorologist John Adair, a veteran of three decades at the National Weather Service office in southern Nevada.\n\nTuesday's high temperature tied the mark of four straight days above 115\u00b0F (46.1\u00b0C) set in July 2005. And Adair said the record could be extended through Friday.\n\nAlyse Sobosan said this July has been the hottest in the 15 years she has lived in Las Vegas. A counselor at a charter school that's on summer break, Sobosan said she doesn't step outside during the day if she can help it, and waits until 9 p.m. or later to walk her dogs.\n\n\"It's oppressively hot,\" she said. \"It's like you can't really live your life.\"\n\nIt's also dangerously hot, health officials have emphasized.\n\n\"Even people of average age who are seemingly healthy can suffer heat illness when it's so hot it's hard for your body to cool down,\" said Alexis Brignola, an epidemiologist at the Southern Nevada Health District.\n\nThe searing heat wave gripping large parts of the US also led to record daily high temperatures in Oregon, where it is suspected to have caused six deaths, the state medical examiner's office said Tuesday. More than 161 million people around the US were under heat alerts Tuesday, especially in Western states.\n\nDozens of locations across the West tied or broke previous heat records over the weekend and are expected to keep doing so all week.\n\nThe heat was blamed for a motorcyclist's death over the weekend in Death Valley National Park. At Death Valley on Tuesday, tourists queued for photos in front of a giant thermometer that was reading 120\u00b0F (48.9\u00b0C).\n\nSimon Pell and Lisa Gregory from London left their air-conditioned RV to experience a mid-day blast of heat that would be unthinkable back home.\n\n\"I don't need a thermometer to tell me that it's hot,\" Pell said. \"You hear about it in stories and wildlife documentaries. But just for me, I wanted to experience what it would feel like.... It's an incredible experience.\"\n\nDeath Valley is considered one of the most extreme environments in the world. The hottest temperature ever officially recorded on Earth was 134 F (56.67 C) in July 1913 in Death Valley, though some experts dispute that measurement and say the real record was 130\u00b0F (54.4\u00b0C), recorded there in July 2021.\n\nRecord highs for the date were also hit Tuesday in parts of Oregon and Washington, with Portland reaching 103 F (39.4 C) and Salem and Eugene hitting 105 F (40.5\u00b0C). Triple digit temperatures were also recorded in Idaho.\n\nPhoenix, which has averaged the hottest temperature ever for the first eight days of July in records dating to 1885, tied the daily record Tuesday of 116\u00b0F (46.6) set in 1958.\n\nThe high Tuesday of 106\u00b0F (41.1\u00b0C) in Reno, Nevada, broke the daily record of 104\u00b0F (40\u00b0C) last tied in 2017 and extended to four days the longest streak ever of 105\u00b0F (40.5\u00b0C) or higher. Before this week, at an elevation of 4,500 feet (1,372 meters), the city had never been that hot for more than two consecutive days in records dating to 1888.\n\nThe US heat wave came as the global temperature in June was a record warm for the 13th straight month and marked the 12th straight month that the world was 1.5 degrees Celsius (2.7 degrees Fahrenheit) warmer than pre-industrial times, the European climate service Copernicus said. Most of this heat, trapped by human-caused climate change, is from long-term warming from greenhouse gases emitted by the burning of coal, oil and natural gas, scientists say.\n\nIn Las Vegas, hotels and casinos keep their visitors cool with massive AC units. But for homeless residents and others without access to safe environments, officials have set up emergency cooling centers at community centers across southern Nevada.\n\nFirefighters in Henderson, Nevada, last week became the first in the region to deploy what city spokesperson Madeleine Skains called \"polar pods\" used to cool a person exhibiting symptoms of heat stroke or a related medical emergency.\n\nSkains said four vehicles, including battalion chiefs in the city of more than 330,000 residents have the devices that are similar to units first put into use a month ago in Phoenix. They can be filled with water and ice to immerse a medical patient in cold water on the way to a hospital.\n\nExtreme heat and a longstanding drought in the West has also dried out vegetation that fuels wildfires.\n\nA new blaze in Oregon, dubbed the Larch Creek Fire, quickly grew to more than 5 square miles (12 square kilometers) Tuesday evening as flames tore through grassland in Wasco County. Evacuations were ordered for remote homes about 15 miles (24 kilometers) south of The Dalles.\n\nIn California, firefighters were battling least 18 wildfires Tuesday, including a 42-square-mile (109-square-kilometer) blaze that prompted evacuation orders for about 200 residences in the mountains of Santa Barbara County. The Lake Fire was only 16% contained, and forecasters warned of a \"volatile combination\" of high heat, low humidity and northwest winds developing late in the day.\n\nNortheast of Los Angeles, the 2-square-mile (5-square-kilometer) Vista Fire chewed through trees in the San Bernardino National Forest and sent up a huge plume of smoke visible across the region.\n\nThe National Weather Service said it was extending the excessive heat warnings across most of the Southwest US through Saturday morning.\n\n\"It's not over yet,\" the service in Reno said.", "source": {"uri": {"uri": "8218891902", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-10", "time": "14:31:08", "dateTime": "2024-07-10T14:31:08Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-10T14:30:52Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9450980424880981, "url": "https://www.ntd.com/las-vegas-eyes-record-of-5th-consecutive-day-over-115-degrees-as-heat-wave-continues-to-scorch-us_1004064.html", "title": "Las Vegas Eyes Record of 5th Consecutive Day Over 115 Degrees as Heat Wave Continues to Scorch US", "body": "LAS VEGAS -- Used to shrugging off the heat, Las Vegas residents are now eyeing the thermometer as the desert city is on track Wednesday to set a record for the most consecutive days over 115 degrees Fahrenheit (46.1 Celsius) amid a lingering hot spell that will continue scorching much of the U.S. into the weekend.\n\nOn Tuesday, Las Vegas flirted again with the all-time temperature record of 120 F (48.8 C) reached on Sunday, but settled for a new daily mark of 119 F (48.3 C) that smashed the old one of 116 F (46.6 C) set for the date in 2021. Forecasters say the city will likely hit a record fifth straight day above 115 F (46.1 C) on Wednesday.\n\nEven by desert standards, the prolonged baking that Nevada's largest city is experiencing is nearly unprecedented.\n\n\"This is the most extreme heat wave in the history of record-keeping in Las Vegas since 1937,\" said meteorologist John Adair, a veteran of three decades at the National Weather Service office in southern Nevada.\n\nTuesday's high temperature tied the mark of four straight days above 115 F (46.1 C) set in July 2005. And Mr. Adair said the record could be extended through Friday.\n\nAlyse Sobosan said this July has been the hottest in the 15 years she has lived in Las Vegas. A counselor at a charter school that's on summer break, Ms. Sobosan said she doesn't step outside during the day if she can help it, and waits until 9 p.m. or later to walk her dogs.\n\n\"It's oppressively hot,\" she said. \"It's like you can't really live your life.\"\n\nIt's also dangerously hot, health officials have emphasized.\n\n\"Even people of average age who are seemingly healthy can suffer heat illness when it's so hot its hard for your body to cool down,\" said Alexis Brignola, an epidemiologist at the Southern Nevada Health District.\n\nThe searing heat wave gripping large parts of the U.S. also led to record daily high temperatures in Oregon, where it is suspected to have caused six deaths, the state medical examiner's office said Tuesday. More than 161 million people around the U.S. were under heat alerts Tuesday, especially in Western states.\n\nDozens of locations across the West tied or broke previous heat records over the weekend and are expected to keep doing so all week.\n\nThe heat was blamed for a motorcyclist's death over the weekend in Death Valley National Park. At Death Valley on Tuesday, tourists queued for photos in front of a giant thermometer that was reading 120 F (48.9 C).\n\nSimon Pell and Lisa Gregory from London left their air-conditioned RV to experience a mid-day blast of heat that would be unthinkable back home.\n\n\"I don't need a thermometer to tell me that it's hot,\" Mr. Pell said. \"You hear about it in stories and and wildlife documentaries. But just for me, I wanted to experience what it would feel like. ... It's an incredible experience.\"\n\nDeath Valley is considered one of the most extreme environments in the world. The hottest temperature ever officially recorded on Earth was 134 F (56.67 C) in July 1913 in Death Valley, though some experts dispute that measurement and say the real record was 130 F (54.4 C), recorded there in July 2021.\n\nRecord highs for the date were also hit Tuesday in parts of Oregon and Washington, with Portland reaching 103 F (39.4 C) and Salem and Eugene hitting 105 F (40.5 C). Triple digit temperatures were also recorded in Idaho.\n\nPhoenix, which has averaged the hottest temperature ever for the first eight days of July in records dating to 1885, tied the daily record Tuesday of 116 F (46.6) set in 1958.\n\nThe high Tuesday of 106 F (41.1 C) in Reno, Nevada, broke the daily record of 104 F (40 C) last tied in 2017 and extended to four days the longest streak ever of 105 F (40.5 C) or higher. Before this week, at an elevation of 4,500 feet (1,372 meters), the city had never been that hot for more than two consecutive days in records dating to 1888.\n\nIn Las Vegas, hotels and casinos keep their visitors cool with massive AC units. But for homeless residents and others without access to safe environments, officials have set up emergency cooling centers at community centers across southern Nevada.\n\nFirefighters in Henderson, Nevada, last week became the first in the region to deploy what city spokesperson Madeleine Skains called \"polar pods\" used to cool a person exhibiting symptoms of heat stroke or a related medical emergency.\n\nMs. Skains said four vehicles, including battalion chiefs in the city of more than 330,000 residents have the devices that are similar to units first put into use a month ago in Phoenix. They can be filled with water and ice to immerse a medical patient in cold water on the way to a hospital.\n\nExtreme heat and a longstanding drought in the West has also dried out vegetation that fuels wildfires.\n\nA new blaze in Oregon, dubbed the Larch Creek Fire, quickly grew to more than 5 square miles (12 square kilometers) Tuesday evening as flames tore through grassland in Wasco County. Evacuations were ordered for remote homes about 15 miles (24 kilometers) south of The Dalles.\n\nIn California, firefighters were battling least 18 wildfires Tuesday, including a 42-square-mile (109-square-kilometer) blaze that prompted evacuation orders for about 200 residences in the mountains of Santa Barbara County. The Lake Fire was only 16 percent contained, and forecasters warned of a \"volatile combination\" of high heat, low humidity, and northwest winds developing late in the day.\n\nNortheast of Los Angeles, the 2-square-mile (5-square-kilometer) Vista Fire chewed through trees in the San Bernardino National Forest and sent up a huge plume of smoke visible across the region.\n\nThe National {"uri": "8218080934", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-10", "time": "05:51:25", "dateTime": "2024-07-10T05:51:25Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-10T05:49:40Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.929411768913269, "url": "https://www.dailymail.co.uk/wires/ap/article-13618687/Las-Vegas-eyes-record-5th-consecutive-day-115-degrees-heat-wave-continues-scorch-US.html", "title": "Las Vegas eyes record of 5th consecutive day over 115 degrees as...", "body": "LAS VEGAS (AP) - Used to shrugging off the heat, Las Vegas residents are now eyeing the thermometer as the desert city is on track Wednesday to set a record for the most consecutive days over 115 degrees Fahrenheit (46.1 Celsius) amid a lingering hot spell that will continue scorching much of the U.S. into the weekend.\n\nOn Tuesday, Las Vegas flirted again with the all-time temperature record of 120 F (48.8 C) reached on Sunday, but settled for a new daily mark of 119 F (48.3 C) that smashed the old one of 116 F (46.6 C) set for the date in 2021. Forecasters say the city will likely hit a record fifth straight day above 115 F (46.1 C) on Wednesday.\n\nEven by desert standards, the prolonged baking that Nevada\u00b4s largest city is experiencing is nearly unprecedented.\n\n\"This is the most extreme heat wave in the history of record-keeping in Las Vegas since 1937,\" said meteorologist John Adair, a veteran of three decades at the National Weather Service office in southern Nevada.\n\nTuesday's high temperature tied the mark of four straight days above 115 F (46.1 C) set in July 2005. And Adair said the record could be extended through Friday.\n\nAlyse Sobosan said this July has been the hottest in the 15 years she has lived in Las Vegas. A counselor at a charter school that's on summer break, Sobosan said she doesn\u00b4t step outside during the day if she can help it, and waits until 9 p.m. or later to walk her dogs.\n\nFILE - People cool off in misters along the Las Vegas Strip, Sunday, July 7, 2024, in Las Vegas. Used to shrugging off the heat, Las Vegas residents are now eyeing the thermometer as the desert city is on track Wednesday to set a record for the most consecutive days over 115 degrees (46.1 C) amid a lingering hot spell that's expected to continue scorching much of the U.S. into the weekend. (AP Photo/John Locher, File)\n\n\"It\u00b4s oppressively hot,\" she said. \"It\u00b4s like you can\u00b4t really live your life.\"\n\nIt's also dangerously hot, health officials have emphasized.\n\n\"Even people of average age who are seemingly healthy can suffer heat illness when it\u00b4s so hot its hard for your body to cool down,\" said Alexis Brignola, an epidemiologist at the Southern Nevada Health District.\n\nThe searing heat wave gripping large parts of the U.S. also led to record daily high temperatures in Oregon, where it is suspected to have caused six deaths, the state medical examiner\u00b4s office said Tuesday. More than 161 million people around the U.S. were under heat alerts Tuesday, especially in Western states.\n\nDozens of locations across the West tied or broke previous heat records over the weekend and are expected to keep doing so all week.\n\nThe heat was blamed for a motorcyclist\u00b4s death over the weekend in Death Valley National Park. At Death Valley on Tuesday, tourists queued for photos in front of a giant thermometer that was reading 120 F (48.9 C).\n\nSimon Pell and Lisa Gregory from London left their air-conditioned RV to experience a mid-day blast of heat that would be unthinkable back home.\n\n\"I don't need a thermometer to tell me that it's hot,\" Pell said. \"You hear about it in stories and and wildlife documentaries. But just for me, I wanted to experience what it would feel like. ... It's an incredible experience.\"\n\nDeath Valley is considered one of the most extreme environments in the world. The hottest temperature ever officially recorded on Earth was 134 F (56.67 C) in July 1913 in Death Valley, though some experts dispute that measurement and say the real record was 130 F (54.4 C), recorded there in July 2021.\n\nRecord highs for the date were also hit Tuesday in parts of Oregon and Washington, with Portland reaching 103 F (39.4 C) and Salem and Eugene hitting 105 F (40.5 C). Triple digit temperatures were also recorded in Idaho.\n\nPhoenix, which has averaged the hottest temperature ever for the first eight days of July in records dating to 1885, tied the daily record Tuesday of 116 F (46.6) set in 1958.\n\nThe high Tuesday of 106 F (41.1 C) in Reno, Nevada, broke the daily record of 104 F (40 C) last tied in 2017 and extended to four days the longest streak ever of 105 F (40.5 C) or higher. Before this week, at an elevation of 4,500 feet (1,372 meters), the city had never been that hot for more than two consecutive days in records dating to 1888.\n\nThe U.S. heat wave came as the global temperature in June was a record warm for the 13th straight month and marked the 12th straight month that the world was 1.5 degrees Celsius (2.7 degrees Fahrenheit) warmer than pre-industrial times, the European climate service Copernicus said. Most of this heat, trapped by human-caused climate change, is from long-term warming from greenhouse gases emitted by the burning of coal, oil and natural gas, scientists say.\n\nIn Las Vegas, hotels and casinos keep their visitors cool with massive AC units. But for homeless residents and others without access to safe environments, officials have set up emergency cooling centers at community centers across southern Nevada.\n\nFirefighters in Henderson, Nevada, last week became the first in the region to deploy what city spokesperson Madeleine Skains called \"polar pods\" used to cool a person exhibiting symptoms of heat stroke or a related medical emergency.\n\nSkains said four vehicles, including battalion chiefs in the city of more than 330,000 residents have the devices that are similar to units first put into use a month ago in Phoenix. They can be filled with water and ice to immerse a medical patient in cold water on the way to a hospital.\n\nExtreme heat and a longstanding drought in the West has also dried out vegetation that fuels wildfires.\n\nA new blaze in Oregon, dubbed the Larch Creek Fire, quickly grew to more than 5 square miles (12 square kilometers) Tuesday evening as flames tore through grassland in Wasco County. Evacuations were ordered for remote homes about 15 miles (24 kilometers) south of The Dalles.\n\nIn California, firefighters were battling least 18 wildfires Tuesday, including a 42-square-mile (109-square-kilometer) blaze that prompted evacuation orders for about 200 residences in the mountains of Santa Barbara County. The Lake Fire was only 16% contained, and forecasters warned of a \"volatile combination\" of high heat, low humidity and northwest winds developing late in the day.\n\nNortheast of Los Angeles, the 2-square-mile (5-square-kilometer) Vista Fire chewed through trees in the San Bernardino National Forest and sent up a huge plume of smoke visible across the region.\n\nThe National Weather Service said it was extending the excessive heat warnings across most of the Southwest U.S. through Saturday morning.\n\n\"It's not over yet,\" the service in Reno said.\n\nWeber reported from Los Angeles. AP journalists Anita Snow in Phoenix; Ty O'Neil in Death Valley, California; Rio Yamat in Las Vegas; and Scott Sonner and Gabe Stern in Reno, Nevada, contributed to this report.\n\nFILE - People shield their eyes from the sun along the Las Vegas Strip, Sunday, July 7, 2024, in Las Vegas. {"uri": "2024-07-417428134", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-10", "time": "12:34:17", "dateTime": "2024-07-10T12:34:17Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-10T12:19:00Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.929411768913269, "url": "https://internewscast.com/news/las-vegas-aims-for-new-high-temperature-record-with-5th-day-above-115-degrees-in-ongoing-heat-wave-across-the-us/", "title": "Las Vegas aims for new high temperature record with 5th day above 115 degrees in ongoing heat wave across the US - Internewscast Journal", "body": "Las Vegas residents are accustomed to dealing with high temperatures, but they are now closely watching the thermometer as the city is poised to break a record on Wednesday. This record pertains to the number of consecutive days where the temperature has exceeded 115 degrees Fahrenheit (46.1 Celsius). The scorching heatwave affecting Las Vegas is not limited to the city alone, as much of the United States will continue to experience high temperatures through the weekend.\n\nOn Tuesday, Las Vegas flirted again with the all-time temperature record of 120 F (48.8 C) reached on Sunday, but settled for a new daily mark of 119 F (48.3 C) that smashed the old one of 116 F (46.6 C) set for the date in 2021. Forecasters say the city will likely hit a record fifth straight day above 115 F (46.1 C) on Wednesday.\n\nEven by desert standards, the prolonged baking that Nevada's largest city is experiencing is nearly unprecedented.\n\n\"This is the most extreme heat wave in the history of record-keeping in Las Vegas since 1937,\" said meteorologist John air, a veteran of three decades at the National Weather Service office in southern Nevada.\n\nTuesday's high temperature tied the mark of four straight days above 115 F (46.1 C) set in July 2005. And air said the record could be extended through Friday.\n\nAlyse Sobosan said this July has been the hottest in the 15 years she has lived in Las Vegas. A counselor at a charter school that's on summer break, Sobosan said she doesn't step outside during the day if she can help it, and waits until 9 p.m. or later to walk her dogs.\n\n\"It's oppressively hot,\" she said. \"It's like you can't really live your life.\"\n\nIt's also dangerously hot, health officials have emphasized.\n\n\"Even people of average age who are seemingly healthy can suffer heat illness when it's so hot its hard for your body to cool down,\" said Alexis Brignola, an epidemiologist at the Southern Nevada Health District.\n\nThe searing heat wave gripping large parts of the U.S. also led to record daily high temperatures in Oregon, where it is suspected to have caused six deaths, the state medical examiner's office said Tuesday. More than 161 million people around the U.S. were under heat alerts Tuesday, especially in Western states.\n\nDozens of locations across the West tied or broke previous heat records over the weekend and are expected to keep doing so all week.\n\nThe heat was blamed for a motorcyclist's death over the weekend in Death Valley National Park. At Death Valley on Tuesday, tourists queued for photos in front of a giant thermometer that was reading 120 F (48.9 C).\n\nSimon Pell and Lisa Gregory from London left their air-conditioned RV to experience a mid-day blast of heat that would be unthinkable back home.\n\n\"I don't need a thermometer to tell me that it's hot,\" Pell said. \"You hear about it in stories and and wildlife documentaries. But just for me, I wanted to experience what it would feel like. ... It's an incredible experience.\"\n\nDeath Valley is considered one of the most extreme environments in the world. The hottest temperature ever officially recorded on Earth was 134 F (56.67 C) in July 1913 in Death Valley, though some experts dispute that measurement and say the real record was 130 F (54.4 C), recorded there in July 2021.\n\nRecord highs for the date were also hit Tuesday in parts of Oregon and Washington, with Portland reaching 103 F (39.4 C) and Salem and Eugene hitting 105 F (40.5 C). Triple digit temperatures were also recorded in Idaho.\n\nPhoenix, which has averaged the hottest temperature ever for the first eight days of July in records dating to 1885, tied the daily record Tuesday of 116 F (46.6) set in 1958.\n\nThe high Tuesday of 106 F (41.1 C) in Reno, Nevada, broke the daily record of 104 F (40 C) last tied in 2017 and extended to four days the longest streak ever of 105 F (40.5 C) or higher. Before this week, at an elevation of 4,500 feet (1,372 meters), the city had never been that hot for more than two consecutive days in records dating to 1888.\n\nThe U.S. heat wave came as the global temperature in June was a record warm for the 13th straight month and marked the 12th straight month that the world was 1.5 degrees Celsius (2.7 degrees Fahrenheit) warmer than pre-industrial times, the European climate service Copernicus said. Most of this heat, trapped by human-caused climate change, is from long-term warming from greenhouse gases emitted by the burning of coal, oil and natural gas, scientists say.\n\nIn Las Vegas, hotels and casinos keep their visitors cool with massive AC units. But for homeless residents and others without access to safe environments, officials have set up emergency cooling centers at community centers across southern Nevada.\n\nFirefighters in Henderson, Nevada, last week became the first in the region to deploy what city spokesperson Madeleine Skains called \"polar pods\" used to cool a person exhibiting symptoms of heat stroke or a related medical emergency.\n\nSkains said four vehicles, including battalion chiefs in the city of more than 330,000 residents have the devices that are similar to units first put into use a month ago in Phoenix. They can be filled with water and ice to immerse a medical patient in cold water on the way to a hospital.\n\nExtreme heat and a longstanding drought in the West has also dried out vegetation that fuels wildfires.\n\nA new blaze in Oregon, dubbed the Larch Creek Fire, quickly grew to more than 5 square miles (12 square kilometers) Tuesday evening as flames tore through grassland in Wasco County. Evacuations were ordered for remote homes about 15 miles (24 kilometers) south of The Dalles.\n\nIn California, firefighters were battling least 18 wildfires Tuesday, including a 42-square-mile (109-square-kilometer) blaze that prompted evacuation orders for about 200 residences in the mountains of Santa Barbara County. The Lake Fire was only 16% contained, and forecasters warned of a \"volatile combination\" of high heat, low humidity and northwest winds developing late in the day.\n\nNortheast of Los Angeles, the 2-square-mile (5-square-kilometer) Vista Fire chewed through trees in the San Bernardino National Forest and sent up a huge plume of smoke visible across the region.\n\nThe National Weather Service said it was extending the excessive heat warnings across most of the Southwest U.S. through Saturday morning.\n\nWeber reported from Los Angeles. {"uri": "2024-07-417702758", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-10", "time": "16:35:03", "dateTime": "2024-07-10T16:35:03Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-10T16:20:47Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9254902005195618, "url": "https://www.bradfordera.com/news/nation/las-vegas-eyes-record-of-5th-consecutive-day-over-115-degrees-as-heat-wave-continues/article_48600f68-511d-5fc6-95f8-98d9b9a55989.html", "title": "Las Vegas eyes record of 5th consecutive day over 115 degrees as heat wave continues to scorch US", "body": "LAS VEGAS (AP) -- Used to shrugging off the heat, Las Vegas residents are now eyeing the thermometer as the desert city is on track Wednesday to set a record for the most consecutive days over 115 degrees Fahrenheit (46.1 Celsius) amid a lingering hot spell that will continue scorching much of the U.S. into the weekend.\n\nOn Tuesday, Las Vegas flirted again with the all-time temperature record of 120 F (48.8 C) reached Sunday, but settled for a new daily mark of 119 F (48.3 C) that smashed the old one of 116 F (46.6 C) set for the date in 2021. Forecasters say the city will likely hit a record fifth straight day above 115 F (46.1 C) on Wednesday.\n\nEven by desert standards, the prolonged baking that Nevada's largest city is experiencing is nearly unprecedented.\n\n\"This is the most extreme heat wave in the history of record-keeping in Las Vegas since 1937,\" said meteorologist John Adair, a veteran of three decades at the National Weather Service office in southern Nevada.\n\nTuesday's high temperature tied the mark of four straight days above 115 F (46.1 C) set in July 2005. And Adair said the record could be extended through Friday.\n\nAlyse Sobosan said this July has been the hottest in the 15 years she has lived in Las Vegas. A counselor at a charter school that's on summer break, Sobosan said she doesn't step outside during the day if she can help it, and waits until 9 p.m. or later to walk her dogs.\n\n\"It's oppressively hot,\" she said. \"It's like you can't really live your life.\"\n\nIt's also dangerously hot, health officials have emphasized.\n\n\"Even people of average age who are seemingly healthy can suffer heat illness when it's so hot its hard for your body to cool down,\" said Alexis Brignola, an epidemiologist at the Southern Nevada Health District.\n\nThe searing heat wave gripping large parts of the U.S. also led to record daily high temperatures in Oregon, where it is suspected to have caused eight deaths, the state medical examiner's office said. More than 142 million people around the U.S. were under heat alerts Wednesday, especially in Western states.\n\nOn the other side of the nation, the National Weather Service warned of major-to-extreme heat risk over portions of the East Coast.\n\nAn excessive heat warning remained in place Wednesday for the Philadelphia area, northern Delaware and nearly all of New Jersey. Temperatures were around 90 F (32.2 C) for most of the region, and forecasters warned the heat index could soar as high as 108 F (42.2 C). The warning was due to expire at 8 p.m. Wednesday, though forecasters said there may be a need to extend it.\n\nDozens of locations across the West tied or broke previous heat records over the weekend and are expected to keep doing so all week, although the end of the siege was in sight in some areas.\n\nThe heat was blamed for a motorcyclist's death over the weekend in Death Valley National Park. At Death Valley on Tuesday, tourists queued for photos in front of a giant thermometer that was reading 120 F (48.9 C).\n\nSimon Pell and Lisa Gregory from London left their air-conditioned RV to experience a midday blast of heat that would be unthinkable back home.\n\n\"I don't need a thermometer to tell me that it's hot,\" Pell said. \"You hear about it in stories and and wildlife documentaries. But just for me, I wanted to experience what it would feel like. ... It's an incredible experience.\"\n\nDeath Valley is considered one of the most extreme environments in the world, with a temperature of 130 F (54.4 C), recorded there in July 2021.\n\nRecord highs for the date were also hit Tuesday in parts of Oregon and Washington, with Portland reaching 103 F (39.4 C) and Salem and Eugene hitting 105 F (40.5 C).\n\nThe Oregon State Medical Examiner's Office on Wednesday confirmed two new suspected heat-related deaths, bringing to eight the total number of deaths during the heat-wave. One was an 83-year-old man in Washington County and the other was a 72-year-old man in Multnomah County. Seven of the deaths were men and one was a woman. The youngest was 33 but all of the others were age 64 and older.\n\nTriple digit temperatures were also recorded in Idaho.\n\nPhoenix, which has averaged the hottest temperature ever for the first eight days of July in records dating to 1885, tied the daily record Tuesday of 116 F (46.6) set in 1958.\n\nThe U.S. heat wave came as the global temperature in June was a record warm for the 13th straight month and marked the 12th straight month that the world was 1.5 degrees Celsius (2.7 degrees Fahrenheit) warmer than pre-industrial times, the European climate service Copernicus said. Most of this heat, trapped by human-caused climate change, is from long-term warming from greenhouse gases emitted by the burning of coal, oil and natural gas, scientists say.\n\nIn Las Vegas, hotels and casinos keep their visitors cool with massive AC units. But for homeless residents and others without access to safe environments, officials have set up emergency cooling centers at community centers across southern Nevada.\n\nFirefighters in Henderson, Nevada, last week became the first in the region to deploy what city spokesperson Madeleine Skains called \"polar pods\" used to cool a person exhibiting symptoms of heat stroke or a related medical emergency.\n\nSkains said four vehicles, including battalion chiefs in the city of more than 330,000 residents have the devices that are similar to units first put into use a month ago in Phoenix. They can be filled with water and ice to immerse a medical patient in cold water on the way to a hospital.\n\nExtreme heat in the West has also dried out vegetation that fuels wildfires.\n\nA new blaze in Oregon, dubbed the Larch Creek Fire, quickly grew to more than 5 square miles (12 square kilometers) Tuesday evening as flames tore through grassland in Wasco County. Evacuations were ordered for remote homes about 15 miles (24 kilometers) south of The Dalles.\n\nIn California, firefighters were battling least 19 wildfires Wednesday, including a 45-square-mile (117-square-kilometer) blaze that prompted evacuation orders for about 200 residences in the mountains of Santa Barbara County. The Lake Fire was only 16% contained.\n\nNortheast of Los Angeles, the 3.6-square-miles (9.3-square-kilometer) Vista Fire chewed through trees in the Mount Baldy area of the San Bernardino National Forest and sent up a huge plume of smoke visible across the region.\n\nThe National Weather Service said it was extending the excessive heat warnings across most of the Southwest U.S. through Saturday morning.\n\n\"It's not over yet,\" the service in Reno said.\n\nWeber reported from Los Angeles.
